    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="11d4cd1c8155432db68ef48551731fb4">

        function getParameter(parameterName) {
            let result = null,
                tmp = [];
            location.search
                .substr(1)
                .split("&")
                .forEach(function (item) {
                    tmp = item.split("=");
                    if (tmp[0] === parameterName) result = tmp[1];
                });
            return result;
        }

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            queryParamsDelimiter: encodeURIComponent('?'),

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                let redirectURL = getParameter('redirectUri');
                if(redirectURL == null){
                    redirectURL = encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
                }
                if(redirectURL.indexOf('saml_referrer') < 0){
                    redirectURL += encodeURIComponent(((redirectURL.indexOf(this.queryParamsDelimiter) !== -1) ? '&' : '?') + 'saml_referrer');
                }
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + redirectURL;
            },
            retrieveEntity: function () {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // get the results
                            var results = res.data;
                            return resolve(results);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('retrieve entity error: ' + err);
                        });
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage duo to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {doi: doi, entityid: entityId, entityTitle: entityTitle, eventGroupKey: '4f029502-9dee-4899-a658-ceb4eda87d51'}

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (let i in allSsoInstitutions) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();
                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err){
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

China</p>E-mail: <a class="corr-email" title="Link to email address" href="mailto:ygtang@csu.edu.cn"><span>ygtang@csu.edu.cn</span></a>, <a class="corr-email" title="Link to email address" href="mailto:zguo@uow.edu.au"><span>zguo@uow.edu.au</span></a>, <a class="corr-email" title="Link to email address" href="mailto:wanghy419@126.com"><span>wanghy419@126.com</span></a><a class="moreInfoLink" href="/authored-by/Tang/Yougen">Search for more papers by this author</a></div></span><span class="accordion-tabbed__tab-mobile  accordion__closed"><a href="/authored-by/Ji/Xiaobo" class="author-name accordion-tabbed__control" data-id="a7" data-db-target-for="a7" aria-controls="a7" aria-haspopup="true" id="a7_Ctrl" role="button"><span>Xiaobo Ji</span><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-section_arrow_d"></i></a><span class="comma-separator">,&nbsp;</span><div class="author-info accordion-tabbed__content" data-db-target-of="a7" aria-labelledby="a7_Ctrl" role="region" id="a7">

   <div class="abstract-group ">
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en">
         <h2 id="d72629836" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            
            <p>Structural design and modification are effective approaches to regulate the physicochemical properties of TiO<sub>2</sub>, which play an important role in achieving advanced materials. Herein, a plasma-assisted method is reported to synthesize a surface-defect-rich and deep-cation-site-rich S doped rutile TiO<sub>2</sub> (R-TiO<sub>2–</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>-S) as an advanced anode for the Na ion battery. An amorphous shell (≈3 nm) is induced by the Ar/H<sub>2</sub> plasma, which brings about the subsequent high S doping concentration (≈4.68 at%) and deep doping depth. Experimental results and density functional theory calculations demonstrate greatly facilitated ion diffusion, improved electronic conductivity, and an increased mobility rate of holes for R-TiO<sub>2−</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>-S, which result in superior rate capability (264.8 and 128.5 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 50 and 10 000 mA g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively) and excellent cycling stability (almost 100% retention over 6500 cycles). Such improvements signify that plasma treatment offers an innovative and general approach toward designing advanced battery materials.</p>
            </div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adma201801013-sec-0010">
         
         <p>Sodium ion batteries (SIBs) have attracted great research enthusiasm for scalable stationary energy storage systems due to the low cost and natural abundance of sodium resources, and its similar intercalation mechanism to lithium ion batteries.<span><a href="#adma201801013-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201801013-bib-0001_R_d72629810e518" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - Chem. Rev. Adv. Mater. Adv. Energy Mater. Adv. Mater. Adv. Mater. Adv. Energy Mater.">1</a></span> Nevertheless, the much larger ionic radius of sodium (1.02 Å) than that of lithium (0.76 Å) makes it more difficult to find suitable electrode materials for effective and durable accommodation of sodium ions. Titanium dioxide (TiO<sub>2</sub>) has received enormous attention as a sodium ion battery anode due to its high theoretical capacity (335 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>), suitable sodiation pathways, and low volume expansion upon sodiation.<span><a href="#adma201801013-bib-0002" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201801013-bib-0002_R_d72629810e524" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 2 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">2</a>, <a href="#adma201801013-bib-0003" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201801013-bib-0003_R_d72629810e524" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - J. Mater. Chem. A J. Mater. Chem. A Nano Energy J. Am. Chem. Soc.">3</a></span> The low sodium ion mobility within pristine TiO<sub>2</sub> crystal, however, together with its poor intrinsic conductivity, originating from its large bandgap (3.0 eV), leads to the preferential location of the available active sites for high-rate sodium intercalation in the surface layer, resulting in inferior sodium storage performance.<span><a href="#adma201801013-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201801013-bib-0004_R_d72629810e529" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">4</a>-<a href="#adma201801013-bib-0006" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201801013-bib-0006_R_d72629810e529" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - Small">6</a></span></p>
         
         <p>Very recently, many experimental results have revealed that doping TiO<sub>2</sub> with heteroatoms could introduce sufficient defects and oxygen vacancies, which would result in significantly facilitated intrinsic electronic conductivity.<span><a href="#adma201801013-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201801013-bib-0007_R_d72629810e535" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">7</a>, <a href="#adma201801013-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201801013-bib-0008_R_d72629810e535" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - J. Mater. Chem. A">8</a></span> Consequently, various forms of heteroatom-doped TiO<sub>2</sub> (such as with N,<span><a href="#adma201801013-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201801013-bib-0008_R_d72629810e539" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - J. Mater. Chem. A">8</a>, <a href="#adma201801013-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201801013-bib-0009_R_d72629810e539" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - Electrochim. Acta">9</a></span> B,<span><a href="#adma201801013-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201801013-bib-0007_R_d72629810e541" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">7</a></span> Nb,<span><a href="#adma201801013-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201801013-bib-0010_R_d72629810e544" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">10</a></span> Sn,<span><a href="#adma201801013-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201801013-bib-0011_R_d72629810e546" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - Chem. Commun.">11</a></span> and S<span><a href="#adma201801013-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201801013-bib-0012_R_d72629810e548" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - Adv. Mater.">12</a></span>) have been synthesized, and enhanced sodium storage performance has been demonstrated. Theoretical calculations suggested, however, that only when the concentration of dopant is sufficiently high to continuously induce a vacancy band of electronic states in the bandgap of TiO<sub>2</sub>, can any significant improvement of the electronic conductivity be achieved.<span><a href="#adma201801013-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201801013-bib-0013_R_d72629810e552" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - J. Phys.: Condens. Matter">13</a></span> Otherwise, a low dopant level can only introduce localized oxygen vacancy states, which would destroy the electronic mobility and dramatically impair the efficiency of doping.<span><a href="#adma201801013-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201801013-bib-0014_R_d72629810e554" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - J. Phys. Chem. C">14</a></span> Moreover, it has been shown computationally that deep cation-site doping could narrow the bandgap of TiO<sub>2</sub> to a large extent, resulting in higher intrinsic electronic conductivity.<span><a href="#adma201801013-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201801013-bib-0014_R_d72629810e559" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - J. Phys. Chem. C">14</a>, <a href="#adma201801013-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201801013-bib-0015_R_d72629810e559" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - Appl. Surf. Sci.">15</a></span> The doping depth is always confined to near the surface, however, and the doping concentration is relatively low due to the limited solubility of substitutional dopants in bulk TiO<sub>2</sub>. In addition, relevant work with respect to the effects of deep cation-site doping on the sodium storage performance of TiO<sub>2</sub> is currently lacking. Therefore, it is desirable to achieve high doping concentrations together with deep doping in bulk TiO<sub>2</sub>.</p>
         
         <p>Interestingly, several recent pioneering works have shown that designing materials with amorphous features could accelerate ionic and electronic diffusion.<span><a href="#adma201801013-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201801013-bib-0016_R_d72629810e570" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - Science J. Mater. Chem. A Adv. Mater.">16</a></span> Siegel and co-workers demonstrated that amorphous Li<sub>2</sub>O<sub>2</sub> features ionic conductivity that is larger by 12 orders of magnitude compared to its crystalline counterpart.<span><a href="#adma201801013-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201801013-bib-0017_R_d72629810e576" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - Chem. Mater.">17</a></span> Surface amorphous (SA)-TiO<sub>2</sub>@graphene composite exhibited vastly improved lithium storage performance compared to crystalline TiO<sub>2</sub>.<span><a href="#adma201801013-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201801013-bib-0018_R_d72629810e583" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - Adv. Sci.">18</a></span> Typically, defects and oxygen vacancies would be induced during the introduction of amorphous phase.<span><a href="#adma201801013-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201801013-bib-0018_R_d72629810e585" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - Adv. Sci.">18</a>, <a href="#adma201801013-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201801013-bib-0019_R_d72629810e585" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - J. Am. Chem. Soc.">19</a></span> The defect-rich amorphous phase would improve the solubility of dopant atoms, consequently achieving not only a higher doping level, but also enhanced ion and electron diffusion.<span><a href="#adma201801013-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201801013-bib-0019_R_d72629810e587" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - J. Am. Chem. Soc.">19</a>, <a href="#adma201801013-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201801013-bib-0020_R_d72629810e587" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - Energy Environ. Sci.">20</a></span> Inspired by the above considerations, it is believed that combining a high concentration of deep cation-site dopants with surface amorphization would be a promising strategy to improve the anodic performance of TiO<sub>2</sub> for sodium ion batteries.</p>
         
         <p>Herein, a unique surface-defect-rich and deep-cation-site-rich S doped TiO<sub>2</sub> (R-TiO<sub>2−</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>-S) was synthesized through a simple hydrolysis method followed by a plasma-assisted S doping strategy. An ultrathin amorphous shell generated by Ar/H<sub>2</sub> plasma treatment, followed by a high-concentration (≈4.68 at%) of S and deep S doping, gave rise to greatly improved intrinsic electronic conductivity and enhanced sodium ion diffusion in R-TiO<sub>2−</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>-S. Density functional theory (DFT) calculation has shown that cation doping is more advantageous for reducing the bandgap and accelerating the mobility rate of holes at the same doping concentration than anion doping. The advantages of the designed structure result in high reversible capacity, and outstanding long-term cycling stability and rate capability of R-TiO<sub>2−</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>-S in SIBs.</p>
         
         <p>We first synthesized TiO<sub>2</sub> precursor through a simple hydrolysis method. Then, the TiO<sub>2</sub> precursor was treated with Ar/H<sub>2</sub> plasma to form a thin amorphous TiO<sub>2−</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i> shell. This amorphous shell possesses a large amount of defects (stable Ti<sup>3+</sup> and oxygen vacancies), becoming more accessible to dopant atoms.<span><a href="#adma201801013-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201801013-bib-0019_R_d72629810e627" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - J. Am. Chem. Soc.">19</a></span> S vapor, which is produced by the sublimation of S powder on the upstream side of the furnace (Figure S1, Supporting Information), subsequently doped into the defect-rich and surface loosely packed TiO<sub>2−</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>, ensuring a high S doping concentration (≈4.68 at%) and deep S doping depth, thus forming the yellowish-brown R-TiO<sub>2−</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>-S (<b>Figure</b> <a href="#adma201801013-fig-0001">1</a>). For comparison, light-yellow R-TiO<sub>2</sub>-S (doping content of ≈0.87 at%) and light-gray defect-rich R-TiO<sub>2−</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i> were also prepared in similar way but without plasma treatment (R-TiO<sub>2</sub>-S) or without S doping (R-TiO<sub>2−</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>) (Figure <a href="#adma201801013-fig-0001">1</a>). (The details are included in the Experimental Section in the Supporting Information.)</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adma201801013-fig-0001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/0ad095fb-27cd-452f-9009-eb5bab1a30c7/adma201801013-fig-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/0ad095fb-27cd-452f-9009-eb5bab1a30c7/adma201801013-fig-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/67d67836-a218-4de2-8a4f-85a58e9f5632/adma201801013-fig-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/0ad095fb-27cd-452f-9009-eb5bab1a30c7/adma201801013-fig-0001-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 1<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adma201801013-fig-0001&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadma.201801013" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Synthesis process for R-TiO<sub>2</sub>-S, R-TiO<sub>2−</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>, and R-TiO<sub>2−</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>-S.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>The morphology of all the samples was characterized by scanning electron microscopy (SEM). The pristine R-TiO<sub>2</sub> shows a flower-like morphology with an average particle size of about 500 nm, and the crystal size and morphology are well maintained after Ar/H<sub>2</sub> plasma treatment and S doping (Figure S2a–d, Supporting Information). The transmission electron microscope (TEM) images show that the size of each petal is about 250 nm in length and 50 nm in width (Figure S2e–h, Supporting Information). From the magnified TEM images, we can see that the flower is heavily populated with nanopores about 10–12 nm in size (Figure S3a,b, Supporting Information), which is further supported by the pore size distribution results (Figure S4a,b, Supporting Information). The Brunauer–Emmett–Teller (BET) surface areas for R-TiO<sub>2</sub> and R-TiO<sub>2−</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>-S are 46.33 and 53.15 m<sup>2</sup> g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively. Interestingly, the specific surface area of R-TiO<sub>2−</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>-S increased after plasma treatment, which is in good agreement with Xu et al.'s work.<span><a href="#adma201801013-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201801013-bib-0021_R_d72629810e705" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">21</a></span> They proved that the plasma treatment could effectively endow NiO nanosheets with more exposed surface area per unit mass. Thus, the increased surface area of R-TiO<sub>2−</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>-S in our work may be caused by the surface amorphization with the assistance of plasma. The porous flower-like morphology and higher surface area of R-TiO<sub>2−</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>-S could effectively prevent aggregation and improve the electrode/electrolyte contact, leading to better electrochemical properties.<span><a href="#adma201801013-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201801013-bib-0004_R_d72629810e716" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">4</a>, <a href="#adma201801013-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201801013-bib-0022_R_d72629810e716" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - Adv. Mater.">22</a></span></p>
         
         <p>High resolution TEM (HRTEM) images of all the samples are shown in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#adma201801013-fig-0002">2</a>a–d. R-TiO<sub>2</sub> displays a highly crystalline structure with a lattice d-spacing of 0.16 nm, corresponding to the (220) lattice planes of rutile phase (Figure <a href="#adma201801013-fig-0002">2</a>a). After S doping, a very thin amorphous shell less than 1 nm in thickness appears on the R-TiO<sub>2</sub>-S particles (Figure <a href="#adma201801013-fig-0002">2</a>b). Notably, the Ar/H<sub>2</sub> plasma treatment results in much more obvious amorphous shells (≈3 nm) for R-TiO<sub>2−</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i> (Figure <a href="#adma201801013-fig-0002">2</a>c), and the shell thickness is well retained after the S incorporation (Figure <a href="#adma201801013-fig-0002">2</a>d). The element mapping images show homogeneous distributions of Ti, O, and S elements in the R-TiO<sub>2</sub>-S and R-TiO<sub>2−</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>-S (Figure <a href="#adma201801013-fig-0002">2</a>e,f<sub>3</sub>). The energy dispersive X-ray spectroscopy (EDX) results demonstrate a much higher doping concentration in R-TiO<sub>2−</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>-S (4.79 at%) than in R-TiO<sub>2</sub>-S (0.76 at%), suggesting that the loosely packed defect rich amorphous shell is beneficial for the S diffusion into TiO<sub>2</sub> crystal, consequently resulting in a higher doping concentration in R-TiO<sub>2−</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>-S (Figure S5a,b, Supporting Information).</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adma201801013-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/e849e43c-745c-4437-bd09-432ea8300b36/adma201801013-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/e849e43c-745c-4437-bd09-432ea8300b36/adma201801013-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/ff86f98d-3634-4573-ae16-5c81e93458ab/adma201801013-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/e849e43c-745c-4437-bd09-432ea8300b36/adma201801013-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 2<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adma201801013-fig-0002&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadma.201801013" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">HRTEM images of (a) R-TiO<sub>2</sub>, (b) R-TiO<sub>2</sub>-S, (c) R-TiO<sub>2−</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>, and (d) R-TiO<sub>2−</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>-S. Selected high-angle annular dark field (HAADF) images and the corresponding EDX mapping images for (e–e<sub>3</sub>) R-TiO<sub>2</sub>-S and (f–f<sub>3</sub>) R-TiO<sub>2−</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>-S. XRD patterns for the as-prepared (g) R-TiO<sub>2</sub>-S and (h) R-TiO<sub>2−</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>-S samples with Rietveld refinement. i) S 2p XPS spectra of R-TiO<sub>2</sub>-S and R-TiO<sub>2−</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>-S. The insets in (b) and (d) are the respective line profiles along the red lines.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>The X-ray diffraction (XRD) patterns of all the samples (Figure S6a, Supporting Information) show mixed phases with the main diffraction peaks in accordance with the rutile phase (JCPDS#21-1276, P42/mnm (136)), and a few peaks labeled with stars correspond to the anatase phase (JCPDS#21-1272, l41/amd (141)). The effects of S doping on the phase and crystal structure of the TiO<sub>2</sub> were further investigated by Rietveld refinement, and the lattice parameters are summarized in Table S1, Supporting Information. Interestingly, compared to the pristine R-TiO<sub>2</sub> (<i>a</i> = <i>b</i> = 4.5966 Å and <i>c</i> = 2.9597 Å) (Figure S6b, Supporting Information), the lattice parameters of R-TiO<sub>2</sub>-S are increased (<i>a</i> = <i>b</i> = 4.5982 Å and <i>c</i> = 2.9602 Å), while those for R-TiO<sub>2−</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>-S are decreased (<i>a</i> = <i>b</i> = 4.5939 Å and <i>c</i> = 2.9576 Å) after the different S doping processes (Figure <a href="#adma201801013-fig-0002">2</a>g,h). When S occupies O sites and forms Ti<span class="icomoon"></span>S bonds, the lattice parameters would increase. Deeper substitution on Ti sites, however, would form S<span class="icomoon"></span>O bonds, leading to decreased lattice parameters. Therefore, we can deduce that the S atoms mainly occupy the O sites in R-TiO<sub>2</sub>-S, while they tend to replace Ti on the deeper Ti sites in R-TiO<sub>2−</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>-S after the Ar/H<sub>2</sub> plasma treatment. Raman spectra of all the samples show typical Raman modes of TiO<sub>2</sub> (Figure S6c, Supporting Information).<span><a href="#adma201801013-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201801013-bib-0004_R_d72629810e865" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">4</a></span> The obvious shifting and broadening of the Raman peaks of R-TiO<sub>2−</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i> and R-TiO<sub>2−</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>-S indicate that the original symmetry of the TiO<sub>2</sub> lattice is reduced, which may be due to the introduction of defects and the surface amorphization after the Ar/H<sub>2</sub> plasma treatment.<span><a href="#adma201801013-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201801013-bib-0023_R_d72629810e880" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces J. Power Sources">23</a></span></p>
         
         <p>The surface chemical states of all the samples were further investigated by X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS) measurements. The survey XPS spectra (Figure S6d, Supporting Information) indicate the successful doping of S into R-TiO<sub>2−</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>-S and R-TiO<sub>2</sub>-S. Ti<sup>3+</sup> 2p<sub>3/2</sub> (456.6 eV) and Ti<sup>3+</sup> 2p<sub>1/2</sub> (461.9 eV) peaks appear in the Ti 2p spectrum for R-TiO<sub>2−</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>, verifying the introduction of Ti<sup>3+</sup> by plasma treatment (Figure S6e, Supporting Information).<span><a href="#adma201801013-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201801013-bib-0024_R_d72629810e905" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">24</a></span> The Ti 2p and O 1s peaks are shifted to lower energy states, suggesting the introduction of defects as a result of S doping and Ar/H<sub>2</sub> plasma treatment (Figure S6e,S6f, Supporting Information).<span><a href="#adma201801013-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201801013-bib-0004_R_d72629810e910" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">4</a></span> It is seen that both S<sup>2−</sup> and S<sup>4+</sup> species are observed in the S 2p spectra of R-TiO<sub>2−</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>-S and R-TiO<sub>2</sub>-S (Figure <a href="#adma201801013-fig-0002">2</a>i).<span><a href="#adma201801013-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201801013-bib-0012_R_d72629810e925" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - Adv. Mater.">12</a></span> In the case of R-TiO<sub>2</sub>-S, the dominant S state is the S<sup>2−</sup> species (161.7 and 164.0 eV), corresponding to the replacement of O<sup>2−</sup> by S<sup>2−</sup> in the surface, while most of the S states change into S<sup>4+</sup> ions (168.8 eV) in the R-TiO<sub>2−</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>-S after plasma treatment.<span><a href="#adma201801013-bib-0025" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201801013-bib-0025_R_d72629810e942" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 25 - J. Mol. Catal. A: Chem.">25</a></span> As is well known, the S<sup>4+</sup> in the samples may originate from possible surface absorbed SO<sub>3</sub><sup>2−</sup> or S substitution on Ti<sup>4+</sup> sites in the crystal. To distinguish the S state, according to the method in reference,<span><a href="#adma201801013-bib-0026" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201801013-bib-0026_R_d72629810e952" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 26 - Appl. Catal., A">26</a></span> we thoroughly rinsed the R-TiO<sub>2−</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>-S sample with diluted HCl and retested the XPS spectrum of S 2p. After rinsing, the dominant state of S is still S<sup>4+</sup>, although the peak intensity was slightly decreased, demonstrating that the S mainly replaced the Ti<sup>4+</sup> in R-TiO<sub>2−</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>-S, further confirming that the change in the XRD results is caused by the different doping sites in these two samples (Figure S7, Supporting Information).<span><a href="#adma201801013-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201801013-bib-0012_R_d72629810e967" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - Adv. Mater.">12</a></span> The quantitative analysis results demonstrate that the S contents in R-TiO<sub>2−</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>-S and R-TiO<sub>2</sub>-S are 4.68 at% and 0.87 at%, respectively. It has been proved that S substitution for Ti<sup>4+</sup> ions in the TiO<sub>2</sub> crystal is more difficult than the substitution for O<sup>2−</sup> ions.<span><a href="#adma201801013-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201801013-bib-0015_R_d72629810e982" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - Appl. Surf. Sci.">15</a>, <a href="#adma201801013-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201801013-bib-0020_R_d72629810e982" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - Energy Environ. Sci.">20</a></span> In our case, the concentration of S greatly increases, and the S doping is deeper, revealing much easier doping of S into TiO<sub>2</sub> after Ar/H<sub>2</sub> plasma treatment.</p>
         
         <p>Time-of-flight–secondary ion mass spectrometry (TOF-SIMS) measurements were applied to detect the presence and the distribution of S element in all the samples. As shown in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#adma201801013-fig-0003">3</a>a, R-TiO<sub>2</sub> and R-TiO<sub>2−</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i> show no signal of S element, indicating the absence of S in those two samples. For R-TiO<sub>2</sub>-S, the signal intensity of S is definitely present, although it remains steady as the sputtering depth increases, verifying that the S species are almost entirely confined to the surface.<span><a href="#adma201801013-bib-0027" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201801013-bib-0027_R_d72629810e1005" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 27 - Chem. Mater.">27</a></span> In the case of R-TiO<sub>2−</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>-S, the signal intensity of S is significantly increased, suggesting a higher concentration of S. Moreover, the intensity rises as the sputtering depth increases, indicating the deeper doping depth of S in R-TiO<sub>2−</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>-S.<span><a href="#adma201801013-bib-0027" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201801013-bib-0027_R_d72629810e1015" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 27 - Chem. Mater.">27</a></span> All these measurements confirm that a higher concentration and deeper doping of S are achieved in R-TiO<sub>2−</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>-S compared to R-TiO<sub>2</sub>-S sample.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adma201801013-fig-0003"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/f9b2aa32-cdb0-4cf3-9136-c5574ceda4f2/adma201801013-fig-0003-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/f9b2aa32-cdb0-4cf3-9136-c5574ceda4f2/adma201801013-fig-0003-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/e808078f-5033-4e9c-bb03-df106ae01800/adma201801013-fig-0003-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/f9b2aa32-cdb0-4cf3-9136-c5574ceda4f2/adma201801013-fig-0003-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 3<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adma201801013-fig-0003&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadma.201801013" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) TOF-SIMS depth profiles of S element for all the as-prepared TiO<sub>2</sub> samples. EPR images of (b) R-TiO<sub>2</sub> and R-TiO<sub>2</sub>-S, and (c) R-TiO<sub>2−</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i> and R-TiO<sub>2−</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>-S. d) UV–vis absorption spectra of all the as-prepared TiO<sub>2</sub> samples.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>The presence of defects (Ti<sup>3+</sup> or oxygen vacancies) was further investigated by electron paramagnetic resonance (EPR) measurements (Figure <a href="#adma201801013-fig-0003">3</a>b,c). A signal at about g-factor, <i>g</i> = 2.003, is observed for R-TiO<sub>2</sub>-S, R-TiO<sub>2−</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>, and R-TiO<sub>2−</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>-S, which originates from the unpaired electrons trapped by oxygen vacancies.<span><a href="#adma201801013-bib-0028" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201801013-bib-0028_R_d72629810e1077" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 28 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">28</a></span> As shown, another signal at <i>g</i> = 1.996, corresponding to Ti<sup>3+</sup> species, appears in R-TiO<sub>2−</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>, which can be ascribed to the partial reduction of TiO<sub>2</sub> by Ar/H<sub>2</sub> plasma (Figure <a href="#adma201801013-fig-0003">3</a>c).<span><a href="#adma201801013-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201801013-bib-0024_R_d72629810e1094" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">24</a></span> Previous work has demonstrated that incorporating foreign atoms into metal oxides would modify their defect features.<span><a href="#adma201801013-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201801013-bib-0020_R_d72629810e1096" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - Energy Environ. Sci.">20</a>, <a href="#adma201801013-bib-0029" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201801013-bib-0029_R_d72629810e1096" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 29 - Energy Environ. Sci.">29</a></span> As expected, the content of oxygen vacancies in R-TiO<sub>2−</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i> decreases significantly after S doping, and the signal for Ti<sup>3+</sup> almost disappears, which may be caused by the incorporation of S atoms. We collected ultraviolet–visible (UV–vis) absorption spectra for all the samples, which are shown in Figure <a href="#adma201801013-fig-0003">3</a>d. The absorption sharp edge shows an obvious redshift with a reduced bandgap after plasma treatment and S doping, indicating enhanced electronic conductivity (Table S2, Supporting Information).</p>
         
         <p>To evaluate the effects of the plasma-induced surface amorphous shell, high S doping concentration, and deep doping depth toward improving the sodium storage performance of TiO<sub>2</sub>, the electrochemical properties of all the obtained samples were investigated. The first discharge and charge curves of all the samples at 50 mA g<sup>−1</sup> in the voltage range of 0.01–3.0 V show a similar tendency, with an obvious plateau between 1.0 and 1.2 V, which can be ascribed to the formation of solid-electrolyte interphase films and electrolyte side reactions during the first sodiation process (<b>Figure</b> <a href="#adma201801013-fig-0004">4</a>a).<span><a href="#adma201801013-bib-0030" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201801013-bib-0030_R_d72629810e1119" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 30 - J. Mater. Chem. A">30</a></span> Clearly, all the samples exhibit excellent cycling stability (Figure <a href="#adma201801013-fig-0004">4</a>b), and discharge capacities of 254.2, 192.7, 147.1, and 91.4 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> are obtained after 100 cycles for R-TiO<sub>2−</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>-S, R-TiO<sub>2</sub>-S, R-TiO<sub>2−</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>, and R-TiO<sub>2</sub>, respectively (Figure S8a, Supporting Information). As the current density increases to 200 mA g<sup>−1</sup>, all the samples show a capacity increase in the initial few tens of cycles, and the capacity stays steady subsequently, which can be ascribed to the activation process.<span><a href="#adma201801013-bib-0002" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201801013-bib-0002_R_d72629810e1141" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 2 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">2</a>, <a href="#adma201801013-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201801013-bib-0004_R_d72629810e1141" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">4</a></span> Obviously, both surface-amorphization and S doping have positive effects toward improving the sodium storage performance of TiO<sub>2</sub>, leading to greatly enhanced discharge capacities (223.2 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> for R-TiO<sub>2−</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>-S, 195.4 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> for R-TiO<sub>2</sub>-S, and 174.1 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> for R-TiO<sub>2−</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i> at 200 mA g<sup>−1</sup>), while a discharge capacity of only 68.6 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> is delivered for R-TiO<sub>2</sub> (Figure S8b, Supporting Information).</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adma201801013-fig-0004"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/068f6a2e-4803-4f2f-918c-71ed8836ef89/adma201801013-fig-0004-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/068f6a2e-4803-4f2f-918c-71ed8836ef89/adma201801013-fig-0004-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/d69315c2-d5cf-40d7-8c32-028c6b0c2666/adma201801013-fig-0004-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/068f6a2e-4803-4f2f-918c-71ed8836ef89/adma201801013-fig-0004-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 4<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adma201801013-fig-0004&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadma.201801013" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) The initial charge/discharge curves of all the samples at 50 mA g<sup>−1</sup> in the voltage range of 0.01–3.0 V. b) Cycling performances of all the samples at 50 mA g<sup>−1</sup>. c) Rate performances of R-TiO<sub>2</sub>, R-TiO<sub>2</sub>-S, R-TiO<sub>2−</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>, and R-TiO<sub>2−</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>-S. d) Charge–discharge profiles of R-TiO<sub>2−</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>-S at different rates. e) Cycling performance and Coulombic efficiency of R-TiO<sub>2−</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>-S at 10 A g<sup>−1</sup> over 6500 cycles.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>The impressive sodium storage performance of R-TiO<sub>2−</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>-S is further highlighted by its superior rate capability. As shown in Figure <a href="#adma201801013-fig-0004">4</a>c, R-TiO<sub>2−</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>-S exhibits discharge capacities of 264.8, 243.7, 222.5, 183.8, 162.9, and 138.3 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 50, 100, 200, 500, 1000, and 2000 mA g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively. Even at 5000 and 10 000 mA g<sup>−1</sup>, discharge capacities of 111.3 and 101.9 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> are obtained, respectively, and 257.5 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> discharge capacity is attained when the current density recovers to 50 mA g<sup>−1</sup>, indicating excellent reversibility. R-TiO<sub>2−</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>-S has 6.3 times higher discharge capacity than R-TiO<sub>2</sub> at 10 000 mA g<sup>−1</sup>, although R-TiO<sub>2</sub>-S and R-TiO<sub>2−</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i> only show 2.6 times and 3.2 times higher capacities, respectively. The charge–discharge curves of R-TiO<sub>2−</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>-S at different current densities show little shifting and only a small overpotential as the current density increases, further verifying its superior rate performance (Figure <a href="#adma201801013-fig-0004">4</a>d). Electrochemical impedance spectroscopy results for all the samples (Figure S8c, Supporting Information) demonstrate much lower charge-transfer resistance and sodium ion diffusion resistance for R-TiO<sub>2−</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>-S compared to the other samples, which may be ascribed to the increased electronic/sodium ion conductivities originating from the deep cation-site S doping and surface amorphization. Indeed, the electronic conductivities of R-TiO<sub>2</sub>, R-TiO<sub>2</sub>-S, R-TiO<sub>2−</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>, and R-TiO<sub>2−</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>-S were measured to be 2.78 × 10<sup>−8</sup>, 4.91 × 10<sup>−7</sup>, 2.93 × 10<sup>−7</sup>, and 1.28 × 10<sup>−6</sup> S cm<sup>−1</sup>, respectively, according to the four-point probe measurement results. The rate capability of R-TiO<sub>2−</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>-S here is superior to those of reported samples of pristine TiO<sub>2</sub>, heteroatom doped TiO<sub>2</sub>, and carbon coated TiO<sub>2</sub> with various morphologies (Figure S8d, Supporting Information).<span><a href="#adma201801013-bib-0005" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201801013-bib-0005_R_d72629810e1298" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - Part. Part. Syst. Charact.">5</a>, <a href="#adma201801013-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201801013-bib-0010_R_d72629810e1298" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">10</a>, <a href="#adma201801013-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201801013-bib-0023_R_d72629810e1298" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces J. Power Sources">23</a>, <a href="#adma201801013-bib-0031" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201801013-bib-0031_R_d72629810e1298" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 31 - Adv. Energy Mater. J. Power Sources J. Mater. Chem. A">31</a></span> Note that this present sample is without any carbon modification. The surface amorphization is likely to be one of the crucial factors behind the significantly enhanced rate capability of R-TiO<sub>2−</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>-S, which would be more favorable for sodium ion diffusion and charge transfer at high rates.<span><a href="#adma201801013-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201801013-bib-0018_R_d72629810e1304" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - Adv. Sci.">18</a></span> Moreover, the bandgap of R-TiO<sub>2−</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>-S decreased greatly after plasma assisted S doping, resulting in dramatically increased intrinsic electronic conductivity. The R-TiO<sub>2−</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>-S electrode was sequentially cycled at 10 000 mA g<sup>−1</sup> after the rate test to evaluate its long-term cycling stability at a high rate. Strikingly, it showed a discharge capacity of 128.5 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> with ≈100% Coulombic efficiency over 6500 cycles, proving its excellent tolerance under ultrafast insertion/extraction of sodium ions (Figure <a href="#adma201801013-fig-0004">4</a>e). Compared with most reported TiO<sub>2</sub>-based materials (Table S3, Supporting Information), the as-prepared R-TiO<sub>2−</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>-S electrode shows extraordinary cycling stability. TEM images of R-TiO<sub>2−</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>-S after 1000 cycles still show a well-maintained flower-like morphology, confirming the superiority of this robust 3D structure (Figure S9, Supporting Information).</p>
         
         <p>It has been proved that amorphous characteristics in active materials could accelerate their ionic and electronic diffusion.<span><a href="#adma201801013-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201801013-bib-0018_R_d72629810e1335" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - Adv. Sci.">18</a>, <a href="#adma201801013-bib-0032" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201801013-bib-0032_R_d72629810e1335" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 32 - Nat. Mater.">32</a></span> To verify the effects of the amorphous shell on the sodium ion diffusion, we conducted cyclic voltammetry of R-TiO<sub>2−</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i> (Figure S10a, Supporting Information) and R-TiO<sub>2</sub> (Figure S10b, Supporting Information). R-TiO<sub>2−</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i> shows the typical electrochemical characteristics of TiO<sub>2</sub>-based anodes, with a pair of cathodic/anodic peaks at 0.76/0.84 V, while those for R-TiO<sub>2</sub> are at 0.67/0.86 V. Obviously, the voltage offset (Δ<i>E</i><sub>p</sub>) of R-TiO<sub>2−</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i> is much lower than that of R-TiO<sub>2</sub>, indicating less polarization and faster electrochemical reaction kinetics after the introduction of the amorphous shell. The amorphous shell always possesses higher surface area compared to the crystalline structure, which would provide sodium ion access to the electrolyte/TiO<sub>2</sub> interface.<span><a href="#adma201801013-bib-0033" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201801013-bib-0033_R_d72629810e1364" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 33 - Chem. Mater.">33</a></span> Moreover, this amorphous shell is different from an ordinary amorphous structure. It has abundant defects, which could enhance electron transfer within the material.<span><a href="#adma201801013-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201801013-bib-0017_R_d72629810e1366" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - Chem. Mater.">17</a></span></p>
         
         <p>To understand the difference of S doping concentration and doping site in modifying the electronic structure of TiO<sub>2</sub>, we calculated the total density of states (TDOS) and the projected density of states (PDOS) of pristine, anion-site S doped (S@O), and cation-site S doped (S@Ti) rutile TiO<sub>2</sub> with different doping concentrations using 24-atom (4.17 at%), and 162-atom (0.62 at%) rutile supercells, respectively (Figure S11, Supporting Information). Clearly, the bandgap decreases after both S@O and S@Ti (Table S4, Supporting Information). For S@O, the S 3p states are mixed with the valence band maximum (VBM) owing to the much higher atomic orbital energy level of the S 3p state than that of the O 2p state, thus resulting in an increased bandwidth of the valence band and a narrowed bandgap (<b>Figure</b> <a href="#adma201801013-fig-0005">5</a>a,b). S@Ti does not generate free carriers and the lone pair 3s state is present at the VBM, which also results in a decrease in the bandgap (Figure <a href="#adma201801013-fig-0005">5</a>a,b). The wave functions for the conduction band maximum (CBM) and VBM in 24-atom supercells of pure and S doped rutile TiO<sub>2</sub> show similar results (Figure <a href="#adma201801013-fig-0005">5</a>c–f). As consequence, anion-site and cation-site S doping strategies have two different mechanisms of action on the band structure of rutile phase, between which, cation-site doping is more advantageous for reducing the bandgap at the same doping concentration (Table S4, Supporting Information). Notably, with increasing doping concentration, the bandgap decreases gradually from 1.54 eV (0.61 at% S doping concentration) to 0.98 eV (4.16 at% S doping concentration) (Table S4, Supporting Information). Moreover, the calculated results for the carrier effective masses along different axes in 24-atom supercells of pure and S doped TiO<sub>2</sub> show a greatly decreased hole effective mass after S doping, which would increase the mobility of holes in the rutile and hence increase its intrinsic electronic conductivity (Table S5, Supporting Information). These calculation results indicate that cation-site S doping combined with a high doping concentration could effectively decrease the bandgap and increase the mobility of holes in rutile to a greater extent, thus giving rise to better electronic conductivity.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adma201801013-fig-0005"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/873db91c-8564-4478-8d77-9dbf7af524e7/adma201801013-fig-0005-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/873db91c-8564-4478-8d77-9dbf7af524e7/adma201801013-fig-0005-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/5d07960a-875a-4963-9840-579619475bf8/adma201801013-fig-0005-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/873db91c-8564-4478-8d77-9dbf7af524e7/adma201801013-fig-0005-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 5<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adma201801013-fig-0005&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadma.201801013" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Calculated total density of states (TDOS) and partial density of states (PDOS) of (a) pristine rutile TiO<sub>2</sub> and S doped 162 atom TiO<sub>2</sub> supercells with different doping sites, (b) pristine rutile TiO<sub>2</sub> and S doped 24 atom TiO<sub>2</sub> supercells with different doping sites. The average potentials of the host elements far away from the dopant are used to align the DOS. Square wavefunctions for the conduction band minimum (CBM) and valence band maximum (VBM) in 24-atom supercells of (c,d) pure TiO<sub>2</sub>, (e,f) O-site S doped TiO<sub>2</sub> (S@O), and (g,h) Ti-site S doped TiO<sub>2</sub> (S@Ti) (electron density: 2 × 10<sup>−5</sup> e Å<sup>−3</sup>).</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>In summary, an Ar/H<sub>2</sub> plasma-assisted S doping strategy was designed in this work to modify rutile TiO<sub>2</sub>. By means of prior Ar/H<sub>2</sub> plasma treatment, an ultrathin surface amorphous shell (≈3 nm) was induced, and a much higher S doping concentration and deep S doping (cation site doping) were achieved. When used as sodium ion battery anode, the as-prepared R-TiO<sub>2−</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>-S exhibited significantly improved sodium storage performance compared with other control samples, with high discharge capacities of 264.8 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> and 128.5 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 50 and 10 000 mA g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively, together with almost 100% capacity retention at 10 000 mA g<sup>−1</sup> over 6500 cycles. This plasma-assisted S doped TiO<sub>2</sub> without any carbon coating showed ultrafast sodium insertion/extraction capability and extraordinary cycling stability, which outperformed most reported TiO<sub>2</sub> samples in the references. The experimental measurements and DFT calculations showed that the superior electrochemical performance of R-TiO<sub>2−</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>-S can be attributed to its highly improved intrinsic electronic conductivity and accelerated sodium ion diffusion kinetics originating from the surface amorphization and deep S doping with high doping concentration. Such improvements signify that structural modification by plasma treatment is an effective strategy for improving the electrochemical performance of advanced battery materials.</p>
         </section>
      <div class="article-section__content">
